<div class="ltx_page_content">

# IDOL: Inverse-Dynamics-Guided Future Prediction for End-to-End Autonomous Driving

<div class="ltx_authors">

<span class="ltx_creator ltx_role_author">
<span class="ltx_personname">Chenghao Zhang, Timin Li, Dongmei Li\
Department of Electronic Engineering, Tsinghua University\
<span id="id1.1.id1"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_typewriter">zhangch24@mails.tsinghua.edu.cn,litm22@mails.tsinghua.edu.cn,lidmei@tsinghua.edu.cn</span>
</span></span>

</div>

<div id="abstract1" class="ltx_abstract">

###### Abstract

End-to-end autonomous driving has emerged as a compelling paradigm for
learning planning directly from sensor observations, while recent
world-model-based approaches further enrich this paradigm by enabling
explicit reasoning about how the scene may evolve in the future. Yet
future prediction alone does not guarantee better planning unless the
predicted evolution can be converted into planning-relevant trajectory
updates. Many current methods still forecast future scene states without
explicitly decoding the motion implications hidden in state transitions.
As a result, future reasoning often remains descriptively useful but
only weakly coupled to executable motion generation. To address this
limitation, we propose <span class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold">IDOL</span>,
an inverse-dynamics-guided future prediction framework for
world-model-based end-to-end planning in latent BEV space, where inverse
dynamics serves as the key bridge between future prediction and
trajectory optimization. IDOL first predicts multiple future latent
scene states with a BEV world model, then applies an inverse dynamics
model to adjacent latent futures to decode transition-aware trajectory
features and recover planning-relevant motion deltas that explain how
the latent world evolves over time. These inverse-dynamics-derived
signals are used to optimize the planned trajectory, turning future
forecasting from passive scene anticipation into actionable planning
guidance. A lightweight closed-loop refinement module further improves
long-horizon consistency by reusing the optimized trajectory for another
round of future-aware reasoning. By introducing inverse dynamics into
latent future reasoning, IDOL tightens the coupling between world
modeling and planning. Extensive experiments on the NAVSIM v1 and NAVSIM
v2 benchmarks show that IDOL achieves state-of-the-art performance among
comparable methods.

</div>

<div id="S1" class="section ltx_section">

## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">1 </span>Introduction

<div id="S1.p1" class="ltx_para">

Autonomous driving\[<a href="#bib.bib4" class="ltx_ref"
title="Vad: vectorized scene representation for efficient autonomous driving">27</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib5" class="ltx_ref"
title="Vadv2: end-to-end vectorized autonomous driving via probabilistic planning">5</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib6" class="ltx_ref"
title="Sparsedrive: end-to-end autonomous driving via sparse scene representation">55</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib7" class="ltx_ref"
title="Hydra-mdp: end-to-end multimodal planning with multi-target hydra-distillation">40</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib66" class="ltx_ref"
title="BridgeDrive: diffusion bridge policy for closed-loop trajectory planning in autonomous driving">46</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib67" class="ltx_ref"
title="FlowAD: ego-scene interactive modeling for autonomous driving">14</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib68" class="ltx_ref"
title="OccDriver: future occupancy guided dual-branch trajectory planner in autonomous driving">22</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib69" class="ltx_ref"
title="Plan-r1: safe and feasible trajectory planning as language modeling">58</a>\]
has advanced significantly in recent years, driven by a paradigm shift
from modular perception-prediction-planning pipelines to unified
end-to-end learning frameworks. Recent advances have further expanded
this paradigm toward future-aware, world-model-guided
planning\[<a href="#bib.bib59" class="ltx_ref"
title="ResWorld: temporal residual world model for end-to-end autonomous driving">78</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib53" class="ltx_ref"
title="Drivelaw: unifying planning and video generation in a latent driving world">68</a>\]
,scalable sim-real training\[<a href="#bib.bib60" class="ltx_ref"
title="Simscale: learning to drive via real-world simulation at scale">59</a>\]
, and video-centric pretraining\[<a href="#bib.bib58" class="ltx_ref"
title="Learning to drive is a free gift: large-scale label-free autonomy pretraining from unposed in-the-wild videos">52</a>\]
,leading to markedly stronger robustness and planning performance on
realistic benchmarks such as
nuScenes\[<a href="#bib.bib70" class="ltx_ref"
title="Nuscenes: a multimodal dataset for autonomous driving">2</a>\]
and NAVSIM\[<a href="#bib.bib57" class="ltx_ref"
title="Navsim: data-driven non-reactive autonomous vehicle simulation and benchmarking">9</a>\].
At the same time, multimodal and generative
formulations\[<a href="#bib.bib51" class="ltx_ref"
title="WAM-flow: parallel coarse-to-fine motion planning via discrete flow matching for autonomous driving">70</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib54" class="ltx_ref"
title="GuideFlow: constraint-guided flow matching for planning in end-to-end autonomous driving">45</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib55" class="ltx_ref"
title="Discrete diffusion for reflective vision-language-action models in autonomous driving">35</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib56" class="ltx_ref"
title="DiffE2E: rethinking end-to-end driving with a hybrid diffusion-regression-classification policy">80</a>\]
have shown that planning quality can benefit substantially from moving
beyond deterministic single-pass regression.More recently, VLA-based
systems\[<a href="#bib.bib61" class="ltx_ref"
title="SGDrive: scene-to-goal hierarchical world cognition for autonomous driving">31</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib62" class="ltx_ref"
title="Diffvla: vision-language guided diffusion planning for autonomous driving">25</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib17" class="ltx_ref"
title="Adathinkdrive: adaptive thinking via reinforcement learning for autonomous driving">47</a>\]
extended the scope of future-aware decision making by injecting
language-grounded reasoning, action abstraction, and reflective
post-training into driving systems.

</div>

<figure id="S1.F1" class="ltx_figure">
<img src="2605.31476v1/x1.png" id="S1.F1.g1"
class="ltx_graphics ltx_centering ltx_img_landscape" width="830"
height="315" alt="Refer to caption" />
<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure">Figure 1:
</span>Comparison of three planning paradigms. (a) Traditional methods
directly map sensor inputs to a predicted trajectory. (b)
World-model-based methods additionally predict future latent BEV states
to support planning, but these predicted futures are only loosely
connected to motion generation. (c) In contrast, IDOL further applies an
inverse dynamics model to adjacent future latent BEV states to extract
transition-aware motion cues for trajectory refinement, making future
prediction more actionable for planning.</figcaption>
</figure>

<div id="S1.p2" class="ltx_para">

For trajectory planning, traditional end-to-end autonomous driving
methods\[<a href="#bib.bib63" class="ltx_ref"
title="CausalVAD: de-confounding end-to-end autonomous driving via causal intervention">57</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib64" class="ltx_ref"
title="MeanFuser: fast one-step multi-modal trajectory generation and adaptive reconstruction via meanflow for end-to-end autonomous driving">62</a>\]
typically rely on the current state, which constitutes a form of passive
planning, as illustrated in Figure <a href="#S1.F1" class="ltx_ref"
title="Figure 1 ‣ 1 Introduction ‣ IDOL: Inverse-Dynamics-Guided Future Prediction for End-to-End Autonomous Driving"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">1</span></a>(a). Subsequently, many
approaches\[<a href="#bib.bib40" class="ltx_ref"
title="WPT: world-to-policy transfer via online world model distillation">28</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib65" class="ltx_ref"
title="SafeDrive: fine-grained safety reasoning for end-to-end driving in a sparse world">29</a>\]
have introduced world models into end-to-end planning, adopting the
predict-decide paradigm to make decision-making more foresighted, as
illustrated in Figure <a href="#S1.F1" class="ltx_ref"
title="Figure 1 ‣ 1 Introduction ‣ IDOL: Inverse-Dynamics-Guided Future Prediction for End-to-End Autonomous Driving"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">1</span></a>(b).

</div>

<div id="S1.p3" class="ltx_para">

Yet a fundamental bottleneck remains: even when future prediction is
available, current methods\[<a href="#bib.bib12" class="ltx_ref"
title="End-to-end driving with online trajectory evaluation via bev world model">38</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib13" class="ltx_ref"
title="Future-aware end-to-end driving: bidirectional modeling of trajectory planning and scene evolution">77</a>\]
still leave a weak and poorly interpretable bridge between forecasting
how the scene may evolve and using that evolution to produce dynamically
meaningful trajectory updates. Consequently, predicted futures may be
informative without being sufficiently actionable. The planner may know
what could happen next, yet still lack a principled mechanism for
determining how the current trajectory should be revised so that it
remains executable, dynamically coherent, and robust over long horizons.
This gap becomes especially harmful in safety-critical driving, where
future reasoning must ultimately serve motion generation rather than
remain passive forecasting. Our key insight is that *the missing signal
lies not only in the predicted future states themselves, but more
importantly in the transitions between them*. As illustrated in
Figure <a href="#S1.F1" class="ltx_ref"
title="Figure 1 ‣ 1 Introduction ‣ IDOL: Inverse-Dynamics-Guided Future Prediction for End-to-End Autonomous Driving"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">1</span></a>(c), inverse dynamics provides
a natural way to decode this signal: unlike forward world modeling,
which predicts how the scene will evolve given a motion hypothesis,
inverse dynamics asks the complementary question of *what
motion-relevant change is implied by the evolution from one state to the
next*. In latent Bird’s Eye View space, adjacent predicted futures
encode how the ego vehicle and surrounding scene are expected to evolve
under a particular planning hypothesis, and the transition from one
latent future to the next therefore contains recoverable motion
semantics that implicitly reflect the trajectory adjustment most
consistent with the predicted scene evolution. Rather than treating
future latent states as static planning features, we decode adjacent
latent transitions with an inverse dynamics model to extract
transition-aware trajectory features and infer motion deltas for
planning optimization. In this way, inverse dynamics becomes the
explicit bridge between world modeling and trajectory generation,
turning future prediction from *passive scene forecasting* into
*planning-aware transition reasoning*.

</div>

<div id="S1.p4" class="ltx_para">

Based on this perspective, we propose <span id="S1.p4.1.1"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold">IDOL</span>, a world-model-based
end-to-end planner that operates in latent BEV space and tightly couples
future reasoning with trajectory optimization. IDOL first predicts
multiple future latent scene states with a BEV world model, giving the
planner access to anticipated scene evolution beyond the current
observation. It then applies an inverse dynamics model to each pair of
adjacent latent futures to infer motion-aware transition features and
planning-relevant trajectory deltas that explain the predicted
evolution. These inverse-dynamics-derived signals are aggregated and
injected into the planning process to optimize the anchor trajectory,
enabling the planner to revise its motion according to the dynamics
implied by future state transitions rather than according to future
context alone. A lightweight closed-loop refinement module is further
introduced as a supplementary mechanism to re-evaluate the updated
trajectory and improve long-horizon consistency.

</div>

<div id="S1.p5" class="ltx_para">

In summary, our contributions are as follows:

</div>

<div id="S1.p6" class="ltx_para ltx_noindent">

<span id="S1.p6.1.1" class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold">i.</span> We
identify a fundamental gap in recent end-to-end and world-model-based
planners: current methods still lack a sufficiently explicit and
interpretable mechanism for converting predicted future evolution into
planning-relevant trajectory updates, which limits the actionability of
future reasoning for executable motion generation.

</div>

<div id="S1.p7" class="ltx_para ltx_noindent">

<span id="S1.p7.1.1" class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold">ii.</span> We
propose IDOL, a world-model-based end-to-end planner in latent BEV space
that introduces inverse dynamics into latent future reasoning, thereby
establishing an explicit bridge between world modeling and executable
motion generation. We further design a lightweight closed-loop
refinement module to improve long-horizon consistency through another
round of future-aware reasoning.

</div>

<div id="S1.p8" class="ltx_para ltx_noindent">

<span id="S1.p8.1.1" class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold">iii.</span> We
conduct extensive experiments on both NAVSIM v1 and NAVSIM v2, including
comprehensive comparisons and analysis, and show that our method
consistently achieves state-of-the-art performance among comparable
methods. These results validate the effectiveness of our design in
improving planning quality across different benchmark settings.

</div>

</div>

<div id="S2" class="section ltx_section">

## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">2 </span>Related work

<div id="S2.SS1" class="section ltx_subsection">

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">2.1 </span>End-to-end autonomous driving

<div id="S2.SS1.p1" class="ltx_para">

End-to-end autonomous driving\[<a href="#bib.bib13" class="ltx_ref"
title="Future-aware end-to-end driving: bidirectional modeling of trajectory planning and scene evolution">77</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib14" class="ltx_ref"
title="Hydra-next: robust closed-loop driving with open-loop training">41</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib15" class="ltx_ref"
title="Rap: 3d rasterization augmented end-to-end planning">10</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib16" class="ltx_ref"
title="Navigation-guided sparse scene representation for end-to-end autonomous driving">34</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib17" class="ltx_ref"
title="Adathinkdrive: adaptive thinking via reinforcement learning for autonomous driving">47</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib21" class="ltx_ref"
title="End-to-end autonomous driving: challenges and frontiers">4</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib32" class="ltx_ref"
title="Artemis: autoregressive end-to-end trajectory planning with mixture of experts for autonomous driving">11</a>\]
aims to map sensor observations directly to waypoints, trajectories, or
low-level control actions, thereby avoiding error accumulation and
hand-crafted interfaces in modular perception-prediction-planning
pipelines. Early methods\[<a href="#bib.bib18" class="ltx_ref"
title="Transfuser: imitation with transformer-based sensor fusion for autonomous driving">7</a>\]
largely followed imitation learning, with planning-oriented
formulations\[<a href="#bib.bib19" class="ltx_ref"
title="Ipad: iterative proposal-centric end-to-end autonomous driving">13</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib20" class="ltx_ref"
title="PRIX: learning to plan from raw pixels for end-to-end autonomous driving">67</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib33" class="ltx_ref"
title="Drivesuprim: towards precise trajectory selection for end-to-end planning">73</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib34" class="ltx_ref"
title="Emma: end-to-end multimodal model for autonomous driving">24</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib36" class="ltx_ref"
title="Gen-drive: enhancing diffusion generative driving policies with reward modeling and reinforcement learning fine-tuning">23</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib38" class="ltx_ref"
title="MindDrive: an all-in-one framework bridging world models and vision-language model for end-to-end autonomous driving">54</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib39" class="ltx_ref"
title="ImagiDrive: a unified imagination-and-planning framework for autonomous driving">32</a>\]
later becoming dominant.
TransFuser\[<a href="#bib.bib18" class="ltx_ref"
title="Transfuser: imitation with transformer-based sensor fusion for autonomous driving">7</a>\]
established a strong transformer-based sensor-fusion baseline for
end-to-end driving. UniAD\[<a href="#bib.bib3" class="ltx_ref"
title="Planning-oriented autonomous driving">21</a>\] explicitly
organized perception and prediction around downstream planning,
reframing full-stack driving as a planning-oriented unified
architecture. DriveMamba\[<a href="#bib.bib45" class="ltx_ref"
title="DriveMamba: task-centric scalable state space model for efficient end-to-end autonomous driving">53</a>\]
further improved efficiency and scalability through a task-centric
state-space architecture. LEAD\[<a href="#bib.bib46" class="ltx_ref"
title="LEAD: minimizing learner-expert asymmetry in end-to-end driving">48</a>\]
revisited imitation-based end-to-end driving from the perspective of
learner-expert asymmetry.
DiffusionDrive\[<a href="#bib.bib9" class="ltx_ref"
title="Diffusiondrive: truncated diffusion model for end-to-end autonomous driving">44</a>\]
and GoalFlow\[<a href="#bib.bib8" class="ltx_ref"
title="Goalflow: goal-driven flow matching for multimodal trajectories generation in end-to-end autonomous driving">69</a>\]
moved from deterministic decoding to generative planning, using
truncated diffusion and flow matching, respectively, to better model
multimodal trajectories. In recent work, VLA-based end-to-end
driving\[<a href="#bib.bib49" class="ltx_ref"
title="DriveAgent-r1: advancing vlm-based autonomous driving with active perception and hybrid thinking">81</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib50" class="ltx_ref"
title="NoRD: a data-efficient vision-language-action model that drives without reasoning">51</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib51" class="ltx_ref"
title="WAM-flow: parallel coarse-to-fine motion planning via discrete flow matching for autonomous driving">70</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib52" class="ltx_ref"
title="VGGDrive: empowering vision-language models with cross-view geometric grounding for autonomous driving">61</a>\]
has extended this line of research toward language-grounded action
generation and post-training alignment.
DriveVLA-W0\[<a href="#bib.bib47" class="ltx_ref"
title="DriveVLA-w0: world models amplify data scaling law in autonomous driving">37</a>\]
showed that adding world-model objectives to VLA training supplies dense
supervision and improves scaling with data.
AutoDrive-$`R^{2}`$\[<a href="#bib.bib48" class="ltx_ref"
title="AutoDrive-R2: incentivizing reasoning and self-reflection capacity for vla model in autonomous driving">75</a>\]
introduced chain-of-thought reasoning, self-reflection, and GRPO-based
post-training for physically plausible trajectory generation.Despite
these advances, most end-to-end planners still couple planning primarily
to the current scene or to output-level multimodality, leaving the
connection between future state transitions and action generation
largely implicit.

</div>

</div>

<div id="S2.SS2" class="section ltx_subsection">

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">2.2 </span>World model in autonomous driving

<div id="S2.SS2.p1" class="ltx_para">

World models in autonomous driving\[<a href="#bib.bib23" class="ltx_ref"
title="Drivinggpt: unifying driving world modeling and planning with multi-modal autoregressive transformers">6</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib24" class="ltx_ref"
title="Drivedreamer: towards real-world-drive world models for autonomous driving">64</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib35" class="ltx_ref"
title="Enhancing end-to-end autonomous driving with latent world model">36</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib40" class="ltx_ref"
title="WPT: world-to-policy transfer via online world model distillation">28</a>\]
learn to predict future scene dynamics and are increasingly used for
forecasting, planning, simulation, and long-horizon decision
support.Dreamer\[<a href="#bib.bib25" class="ltx_ref"
title="Dream to control: learning behaviors by latent imagination">15</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib26" class="ltx_ref"
title="Mastering atari with discrete world models">16</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib27" class="ltx_ref"
title="Mastering diverse domains through world models">17</a>\]
introduced latent world modeling for long-horizon planning via imagined
rollouts. GAIA-1\[<a href="#bib.bib10" class="ltx_ref"
title="Gaia-1: a generative world model for autonomous driving">20</a>\]
cast autonomous driving world modeling as action-conditioned generative
sequence modeling over multimodal signals.
Vista\[<a href="#bib.bib11" class="ltx_ref"
title="Vista: a generalizable driving world model with high fidelity and versatile controllability">12</a>\]
advanced controllable driving world modeling with an emphasis on
diversity of environments and
controllability.WoTE\[<a href="#bib.bib12" class="ltx_ref"
title="End-to-end driving with online trajectory evaluation via bev world model">38</a>\]
predicts future BEV states to evaluate candidate trajectories .
SeerDrive\[<a href="#bib.bib13" class="ltx_ref"
title="Future-aware end-to-end driving: bidirectional modeling of trajectory planning and scene evolution">77</a>\]
explicitly coupled future scene evolution and trajectory planning
through iterative bidirectional
refinement.DriveLaW\[<a href="#bib.bib53" class="ltx_ref"
title="Drivelaw: unifying planning and video generation in a latent driving world">68</a>\]
unified video generation and motion planning in a shared latent driving
world so that generator latents directly support closed-loop planning.

</div>

<div id="S2.SS2.p2" class="ltx_para">

Despite this rapid progress, the central difficulty is no longer whether
future states can be predicted, but how those predicted futures
meaningfully shape decisions.several recent
methods\[<a href="#bib.bib29" class="ltx_ref"
title="Driving into the future: multiview visual forecasting and planning with world model for autonomous driving">65</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib28" class="ltx_ref"
title="World4drive: end-to-end autonomous driving via intention-aware physical latent world model">82</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib13" class="ltx_ref"
title="Future-aware end-to-end driving: bidirectional modeling of trajectory planning and scene evolution">77</a>\]
explicitly note that world modeling and planning may be jointly trained.
However, existing work still do not adequately provide a reasonable
explanation for the inherent correlation between future scene modeling
and motion planning. For our setting, this is the unresolved point. We
are interested not merely in predicting future latent BEV states, but in
making those transitions action-relevant, so that planning is
constrained by how the world is expected to evolve and by what control
signal is implied by that evolution.

</div>

</div>

<div id="S2.SS3" class="section ltx_subsection">

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">2.3 </span>Inverse dynamics models

<div id="S2.SS3.p1" class="ltx_para">

Inverse dynamics models\[<a href="#bib.bib44" class="ltx_ref"
title="A recursive lagrangian formulation of maniputator dynamics and a comparative study of dynamics formulation complexity">19</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib41" class="ltx_ref"
title="Inverse and forward dynamics: models of multi–body systems">49</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib42" class="ltx_ref"
title="An inverse dynamics model for the analysis, reconstruction and prediction of bipedal walking">30</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib43" class="ltx_ref"
title="An algorithm for the inverse dynamics of n-axis general manipulators using kane’s equations">1</a>\]
originate from physics, control, and reinforcement learning, where the
goal is to infer the action or control input that caused a transition
between two states.This viewpoint is useful because it makes action
semantics explicit: an inverse dynamics model can identify which aspects
of a transition are action-relevant, recover latent controls when
actions are unavailable or noisy, and encourage representations that
preserve controllable structure rather than merely appearance. Recent
machine learning work\[<a href="#bib.bib30" class="ltx_ref"
title="Predictive inverse dynamics models are scalable learners for robotic manipulation">60</a>\]
has employed inverse dynamics for model-based control, representation
learning, and general sequential decision-making.

</div>

<figure id="S2.F2" class="ltx_figure">
<img src="2605.31476v1/x2.png" id="S2.F2.g1"
class="ltx_graphics ltx_centering ltx_img_landscape" width="830"
height="459" alt="Refer to caption" />
<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure">Figure 2:
</span>Overview of the proposed IDOL framework. (a) The closed-loop
refinement module rolls out future latent BEV states with the BEV world
model and refines the planning query through inverse-dynamics feedback.
(b) The planning network fuses the refined query with trajectory
anchors, predicts candidate trajectories, and selects the best one by
scoring and ranking. (c) The inverse-dynamics-guided refinement network
decodes adjacent future BEV states into spatial and global dynamics
cues, which are fused to update the ego query.</figcaption>
</figure>

<div id="S2.SS3.p2" class="ltx_para">

In autonomous driving, however, inverse dynamics remains comparatively
underexplored. When it appears, it is often auxiliary:
ReSim\[<a href="#bib.bib1" class="ltx_ref"
title="Resim: reliable world simulation for autonomous driving">71</a>\]
uses an inverse dynamics model to convert predicted videos into
executable trajectories and to assess controllability, while
FutureSightDrive\[<a href="#bib.bib2" class="ltx_ref"
title="Futuresightdrive: thinking visually with spatio-temporal cot for autonomous driving">76</a>\]
explicitly frames planning as recovering actions from current
observations and imagined future scenes. These examples are suggestive.
They imply that inverse dynamics can serve as the missing bridge between
“what future state is predicted” and “what action should be taken,”
especially when future reasoning is already available through a world
model. Existing driving world-model methods typically stop at latent
future forecasting, trajectory evaluation, or reward-guided selection;
they rarely learn an explicit transition-to-action mapping over adjacent
predicted states. Our method builds on this observation. By introducing
an inverse dynamics module on top of predicted future latent BEV states,
we make the coupling between state evolution and planning explicit, so
that trajectory generation is aligned not only with anticipated futures
but also with the action-consistent transitions that connect them.

</div>

</div>

</div>

<div id="S3" class="section ltx_section">

## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">3 </span>Methodology

<div id="S3.SS1" class="section ltx_subsection">

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">3.1 </span>Preliminary

<div id="S3.SS1.SSS0.Px1" class="section ltx_paragraph">

##### End-to-end autonomous driving.

<div id="S3.SS1.SSS0.Px1.p1" class="ltx_para">

End-to-end autonomous driving formulates trajectory planning as learning
a direct mapping from raw sensory observations and ego-state inputs to
future ego motion, without relying on a manually designed modular
stack.Recent world-model-based methods further improve this paradigm by
explicitly modeling future scene evolution and using the predicted
futures to guide planning.

</div>

</div>

<div id="S3.SS1.SSS0.Px2" class="section ltx_paragraph">

##### Inverse dynamics model.

<div id="S3.SS1.SSS0.Px2.p1" class="ltx_para">

Inverse dynamics model infers the latent action or motion representation
that explains the transition between two adjacent states. In its general
form, given two consecutive states $`\xi_{t}`$ and $`\xi_{t+1}`$,
inverse dynamics seeks a compact transition descriptor

|  |  |  |  |
|----|----|----|----|
|  | 
``` math
d_{t}=f_{\mathrm{idm}}(\xi_{t},\xi_{t+1}),
``` |  | <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_equation ltx_align_right">(1)</span> |

which captures the motion-consistent change from $`\xi_{t}`$ to
$`\xi_{t+1}`$. This formulation is particularly suitable for autonomous
driving, since adjacent future states naturally encode how ego motion
interacts with scene evolution. In our setting, the two states are
imagined BEV latent states produced by the world model . Therefore,
inverse dynamics provides a natural bridge from *future state
transition* to *trajectory-related motion features*, making it well
suited for refining trajectories from consecutive imagined BEV frames.

</div>

</div>

</div>

<div id="S3.SS2" class="section ltx_subsection">

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">3.2 </span>Scene representation

<div id="S3.SS2.p1" class="ltx_para">

As illustrated in Figure <a href="#S2.F2" class="ltx_ref"
title="Figure 2 ‣ 2.3 Inverse dynamics models ‣ 2 Related work ‣ IDOL: Inverse-Dynamics-Guided Future Prediction for End-to-End Autonomous Driving"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">2</span></a>, our method predicts the final
trajectory by coupling anchor-based planning, latent future simulation,
inverse-dynamics feedback, and candidate selection. We first encode the
current scene with a multi-modal image-LiDAR backbone following
TransFuser\[<a href="#bib.bib18" class="ltx_ref"
title="Transfuser: imitation with transformer-based sensor fusion for autonomous driving">7</a>\],
and obtain a compact BEV representation $`B`$. In parallel, we maintain
an offline trajectory-anchor vocabulary
$`\mathcal{A}=\{\tau_{k}\}_{k=1}^{K}`$, where each anchor
$`\tau_{k}\in\mathbb{R}^{S\times 3}`$ represents a candidate future
motion\[<a href="#bib.bib5" class="ltx_ref"
title="Vadv2: end-to-end vectorized autonomous driving via probabilistic planning">5</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib7" class="ltx_ref"
title="Hydra-mdp: end-to-end multimodal planning with multi-target hydra-distillation">40</a>\].
Each anchor is encoded into an anchor feature $`a_{k}`$, which is
concatenated with the ego-state feature $`z`$ to form an
anchor-conditioned ego query

|  |  |  |  |
|----|----|----|----|
|  | 
``` math
e_{k}^{(0)}=\phi([z,a_{k}]),\qquad k=1,\ldots,K.
``` |  | <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_equation ltx_align_right">(2)</span> |

Here, $`e_{k}^{(0)}`$ serves as the candidate-specific planning query
for downstream future imagination and trajectory refinement.

</div>

</div>

<div id="S3.SS3" class="section ltx_subsection">

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">3.3 </span>Latent BEV world model

<div id="S3.SS3.p1" class="ltx_para">

For each candidate $`k`$, we construct a candidate-conditioned latent
scene by combining the current latent BEV features with the
anchor-conditioned ego query $`e_{k}^{(u)}`$. At rollout step $`u`$, the
ego query is injected into the latent BEV feature map, yielding
candidate-conditioned BEV features $`\tilde{B}_{k}^{(u)}`$. We then form
the scene feature sequence

|  |  |  |  |
|----|----|----|----|
|  | 
``` math
X_{k}^{(u)}=[\,e_{k}^{(u)},\tilde{B}_{k}^{(u)}\,],
``` |  | <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_equation ltx_align_right">(3)</span> |

which is updated by a transformer-based latent world model:

|  |  |  |  |
|----|----|----|----|
|  | 
``` math
\hat{X}_{k}^{(u+1)}=\mathrm{BEVWorldModel}\!\left(X_{k}^{(u)}\right).
``` |  | <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_equation ltx_align_right">(4)</span> |

The first output element is taken as the next ego query
$`e_{k}^{(u+1)}`$, while the remaining features correspond to the next
imagined latent BEV state $`B_{k}^{(u+1)}`$.

</div>

<div id="S3.SS3.p2" class="ltx_para">

To maintain candidate consistency during rollout, the updated ego query
is re-injected into the latent imagined BEV state at the corresponding
future anchor location, producing the next candidate-conditioned BEV
features $`\tilde{B}_{k}^{(u+1)}`$. Repeating this process yields a
candidate-specific latent future trajectory in BEV space,

|  |  |  |  |
|----|----|----|----|
|  | 
``` math
\{(e_{k}^{(u)},\tilde{B}_{k}^{(u)})\}_{u=0}^{U},
``` |  | <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_equation ltx_align_right">(5)</span> |

which is subsequently used by the inverse dynamics module and the
planning head.

</div>

</div>

<div id="S3.SS4" class="section ltx_subsection">

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">3.4 </span>Inverse-dynamics-guided refinement network

<div id="S3.SS4.SSS1" class="section ltx_subsubsection">

#### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">3.4.1 </span>Inverse dynamics model

<div id="S3.SS4.SSS1.p1" class="ltx_para">

While the world model predicts future latent scenes, planning still
requires a mechanism that translates those imagined transitions into
motion-relevant updates. To bridge this gap, we introduce an inverse
dynamics model (IDM). For each pair of adjacent latent imagined BEV
states $`(\tilde{B}_{k}^{(u)},\tilde{B}_{k}^{(u+1)})`$, IDM produces two
complementary outputs:

|  |  |  |  |
|----|----|----|----|
|  | 
``` math
\left(S_{k}^{(u)},\,g_{k}^{(u)}\right)=\mathrm{IDM}\!\left(\tilde{B}_{k}^{(u)},\tilde{B}_{k}^{(u+1)}\right),
``` |  | <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_equation ltx_align_right">(6)</span> |

where $`S_{k}^{(u)}\in\mathbb{R}^{H\times W\times C}`$ is a spatial
dynamics map that preserves position-wise motion variations across the
latent BEV feature map, and $`g_{k}^{(u)}\in\mathbb{R}^{C}`$ is a pooled
global dynamics feature that summarizes the overall transition. When
multiple rollout transitions are available, we aggregate them by
averaging:

|  |  |  |  |
|----|----|----|----|
|  | 
``` math
S_{k}=\frac{1}{U}\sum_{u=0}^{U-1}S_{k}^{(u)},\qquad g_{k}=\frac{1}{U}\sum_{u=0}^{U-1}g_{k}^{(u)}.
``` |  | <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_equation ltx_align_right">(7)</span> |

</div>

<div id="S3.SS4.SSS1.p2" class="ltx_para">

The key idea is that future transitions should provide not only *what*
changes globally, but also *where* critical changes occur in the
imagined scene. A purely pooled inverse dynamics feature is often too
coarse for planning, since only a subset of spatial changes are truly
relevant to the current trajectory hypothesis. By preserving both
spatial and global dynamics, IDM provides a more informative interface
between future imagination and motion refinement.

</div>

</div>

<div id="S3.SS4.SSS2" class="section ltx_subsubsection">

#### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsubsection">3.4.2 </span>Closed-loop query refinement

<div id="S3.SS4.SSS2.p1" class="ltx_para">

We refine the current ego query by combining the spatial and global
dynamics in a dual-branch manner. First, spatial cross-attention lets
the ego query retrieve candidate-relevant local motion evidence from the
imagined BEV transition:

|  |  |  |  |
|----|----|----|----|
|  | 
``` math
\tilde{e}_{k}^{(\ell)}=\mathrm{LN}\!\left(e_{k,\mathrm{in}}^{(\ell)}+\mathrm{CrossAttn}\!\left(e_{k,\mathrm{in}}^{(\ell)},S_{k}^{(\ell)},S_{k}^{(\ell)}\right)\right),
``` |  | <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_equation ltx_align_right">(8)</span> |

where $`\ell`$ indexes the outer closed-loop iteration. We then fuse the
global dynamics feature with the refined query through an
MLN\[<a href="#bib.bib72" class="ltx_ref"
title="Exploring object-centric temporal modeling for efficient multi-view 3d object detection">63</a>\]
for further calibration:

|  |  |  |  |
|----|----|----|----|
|  | 
``` math
\bar{e}_{k}^{(\ell)}=\mathrm{MLN}_{\mathrm{idm}}\!\left(\tilde{e}_{k}^{(\ell)},g_{k}^{(\ell)}\right).
``` |  | <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_equation ltx_align_right">(9)</span> |

This yields a simple but effective dual-branch fusion: the spatial
branch identifies where the relevant future changes occur, while the
global branch provides coarse holistic calibration. As a result, the
feedback passed from imagined futures to planning becomes more spatially
selective and more informative than using only pooled inverse dynamics.

</div>

<div id="S3.SS4.SSS2.p2" class="ltx_para">

To prevent iterative drift, the second and later iterations re-anchor
the current query to the initial anchor-conditioned query:

|  |  |  |  |
|----|----|----|----|
|  | 
``` math
e_{k,\mathrm{in}}^{(\ell)}=\mathrm{MLN}_{\mathrm{cl}}\!\left(e_{k}^{(0)},\bar{e}_{k}^{(\ell-1)}\right),\qquad\ell\geq 1,
``` |  | <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_equation ltx_align_right">(10)</span> |

while for the first iteration we simply set

|  |  |  |  |
|----|----|----|----|
|  | 
``` math
e_{k,\mathrm{in}}^{(0)}=e_{k}^{(0)}.
``` |  | <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_equation ltx_align_right">(11)</span> |

</div>

<div id="S3.SS4.SSS2.p3" class="ltx_para">

After the final refinement iteration, the resulting refined query is fed
into the planning network, where a transformer decoder followed by an
MLP head predicts an offset for each trajectory anchor. The refined
trajectory candidates are then ranked by a reward
model \[<a href="#bib.bib12" class="ltx_ref"
title="End-to-end driving with online trajectory evaluation via bev world model">38</a>\]
to select the final output.

</div>

</div>

</div>

<div id="S3.SS5" class="section ltx_subsection">

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">3.5 </span>Training loss

<div id="S3.SS5.p1" class="ltx_para">

Our network is trained with a mixture of offset regression, reward
supervision, and semantic BEV supervision. Concretely, training combines
a standard winner-take-all trajectory offset regression loss, imitation
reward supervision over the anchor set, simulator-metric reward
supervision, and auxiliary BEV semantic supervision for both the current
and imagined future states. The current and future semantic losses help
stabilize scene encoding and latent future prediction, while the
reward-related losses supervise trajectory ranking in the same manner as
previous methods.

</div>

<div id="S3.SS5.p2" class="ltx_para">

The overall objective is written as

|  |  |  |  |
|----|----|----|----|
|  | 
``` math
\mathcal{L}=\lambda_{\mathrm{off}}\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{off}}+\lambda_{\mathrm{off\text{-}im}}\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{off\text{-}im}}+\lambda_{\mathrm{im}}\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{im}}+\lambda_{\mathrm{sim}}\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{sim}}+\lambda_{\mathrm{map}}\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{map}},
``` |  | <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_equation ltx_align_right">(12)</span> |

where $`\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{off}}`$ denotes the trajectory offset loss,
$`\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{off\text{-}im}}`$ and
$`\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{im}}`$ denote the auxiliary and main
imitation-reward losses, $`\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{sim}}`$ denotes the
simulator-metric loss, and $`\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{map}}`$ denotes the
BEV semantic supervision.

</div>

</div>

</div>

<div id="S4" class="section ltx_section">

## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">4 </span>Experiments

<div id="S4.SS1" class="section ltx_subsection">

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">4.1 </span>Benchmark

<div id="S4.SS1.p1" class="ltx_para">

We evaluate our method on the NAVSIM benchmarks. On NAVSIM
v1 \[<a href="#bib.bib57" class="ltx_ref"
title="Navsim: data-driven non-reactive autonomous vehicle simulation and benchmarking">9</a>\],
we report results on the navtest split using the Predictive Driver Model
Score (PDMS), which aggregates five planning criteria: no-collision
(NC), drivable area compliance (DAC), time-to-collision within bound
(TTC), comfort, and ego progress (EP). We further evaluate on NAVSIM
v2 \[<a href="#bib.bib71" class="ltx_ref"
title="Pseudo-simulation for autonomous driving">3</a>\], including the
challenging navhard split under the two-stage pseudo-simulation
protocol. Its primary metric is the Extended Predictive Driver Model
Score (EPDMS), which extends the evaluation with driving direction
compliance (DDC), traffic light compliance (TLC), lane keeping (LK),
history comfort (HC), and extended comfort (EC), in addition to NC, DAC,
EP, and TTC. All results are reported under the official closed-loop
evaluation protocol.

</div>

</div>

<div id="S4.SS2" class="section ltx_subsection">

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">4.2 </span>Implementation details

<div id="S4.SS2.p1" class="ltx_para">

We use a planning horizon of 4 s with a sampling interval of 0.5 s,
resulting in 8 future waypoints. Our model adopts a ResNet34-based
TransFuser backbone for multi-modal fusion, where the stitched
front-view image input is resized to $`256\times 1024`$ . The latent
feature dimension is set to 256. In the current implementation, the BEV
representation is flattened into 64 latent queries,with
$`H_{\mathrm{BEV}}\times W_{\mathrm{BEV}}=8\times 8`$, and trajectory
planning is performed over 256 trajectory anchors. The resulting model
contains 69.36M parameters and achieves 17.65 FPS on a single NVIDIA RTX
3090 GPU with batch size 1, indicating that IDOL maintains practical
inference efficiency while introducing inverse-dynamics-guided future
reasoning.

</div>

<div id="S4.SS2.p2" class="ltx_para">

Training is conducted on 4 NVIDIA GeForce RTX 3090 GPUs with a batch
size of 4 per GPU for approximately 24 hours. We use AdamW with an
initial learning rate of $`2\times 10^{-4}`$ and weight decay of
$`1\times 10^{-4}`$.

</div>

<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_table">Table 1:
</span>Performance comparison on the NAVSIM v1 navtest split with
closed-loop metrics. “C” and “L” denote camera and LiDAR inputs,
respectively. For fair comparison, all listed results are reported under
the ResNet-34 image-backbone setting. Bold and underlined values
indicate the best and second-best results, respectively.
ResWorld<sup>∗</sup> denotes the historical-frame variant that uses
historical frames to obtain temporal residuals, and DiffE2E<sup>†</sup>
denotes the variant with a ResNet-34 image encoder.</figcaption>
</figure>

</div>

<div id="S4.SS3" class="section ltx_subsection">

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">4.3 </span>Comparison with SOTA

<div id="S4.SS3.p1" class="ltx_para">

Table <a href="#S4.T1" class="ltx_ref"
title="Table 1 ‣ 4.2 Implementation details ‣ 4 Experiments ‣ IDOL: Inverse-Dynamics-Guided Future Prediction for End-to-End Autonomous Driving"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">1</span></a> compares IDOL with recent
end-to-end planners on the NAVSIM navtest split under the
ResNet-34 \[<a href="#bib.bib77" class="ltx_ref"
title="Deep residual learning for image recognition">18</a>\]
image-backbone setting. IDOL achieves the best PDMS of 90.0,
outperforming prior strong baselines.
Table <a href="#S4.T2" class="ltx_ref"
title="Table 2 ‣ 4.3 Comparison with SOTA ‣ 4 Experiments ‣ IDOL: Inverse-Dynamics-Guided Future Prediction for End-to-End Autonomous Driving"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">2</span></a> further evaluates IDOL on the
more challenging NAVSIM v2 navhard split. IDOL achieves the highest
final EPDMS of 38.0 among all comparable methods, substantially
outperforming the strongest baseline WoTE by 10.1 points.

</div>

<div id="S4.SS3.p2" class="ltx_para">

Beyond the main comparisons, Table <a href="#A3.T8" class="ltx_ref"
title="Table 8 ‣ Appendix C Additional evaluation on NAVSIM v2 ‣ IDOL: Inverse-Dynamics-Guided Future Prediction for End-to-End Autonomous Driving"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">8</span></a> reports the extended NAVSIM v2
navtest benchmark. IDOL achieves 89.6 EPDMS, ranking first among learned
methods and remaining competitive with the human agent.
Table <a href="#A3.T9" class="ltx_ref"
title="Table 9 ‣ Appendix C Additional evaluation on NAVSIM v2 ‣ IDOL: Inverse-Dynamics-Guided Future Prediction for End-to-End Autonomous Driving"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">9</span></a> further evaluates the more
challenging navhard stage-1-only setting, where IDOL achieves the best
performance with 76.2 EPDMS, surpassing Hydra-MDP<sup>†</sup> by 3.1
points and WoTE by 9.5 points. Across NAVSIM v1 navtest, NAVSIM v2
navtest, navhard stage-1, and the two-stage navhard evaluation, IDOL
consistently achieves state-of-the-art performance among comparable
learned planners, demonstrating the robustness and stability of
inverse-dynamics-guided future reasoning under both standard and
challenging long-tail driving scenarios.

</div>

<div id="S4.SS4" class="section ltx_subsection">

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">4.4 </span>Ablation study

<div id="S4.SS4.p1" class="ltx_para">

We conduct ablation studies on the NAVSIM navtest split to analyze the
contribution of each design in IDOL. We report PDMS and its five
component metrics. Additional ablation results and analyses are provided
in Appendix <a href="#A4" class="ltx_ref"
title="Appendix D Additional ablation studies ‣ IDOL: Inverse-Dynamics-Guided Future Prediction for End-to-End Autonomous Driving"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">D</span></a>.

</div>

<div id="S4.SS4.SSS0.Px1" class="section ltx_paragraph">

##### Effect of inverse dynamics and closed-loop refinement.

<div id="S4.SS4.SSS0.Px1.p1" class="ltx_para">

Table <a href="#S4.T3" class="ltx_ref"
title="Table 3 ‣ Effect of inverse dynamics and closed-loop refinement. ‣ 4.4 Ablation study ‣ 4 Experiments ‣ IDOL: Inverse-Dynamics-Guided Future Prediction for End-to-End Autonomous Driving"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">3</span></a> studies the main components of
IDOL. Without IDM, future BEV states lack explicit transition-to-motion
feedback, limiting their effect on trajectory refinement. Introducing
IDM improves PDMS from 87.3 to 89.2 by converting latent future
transitions into motion-aware query updates. Closed-loop refinement
further raises PDMS to 90.0 by feeding the updated query back into
future reasoning. The best result is obtained with two iterations, while
three iterations slightly reduce performance, suggesting possible
over-correction.

</div>

<figure id="S4.T3" class="ltx_table">
<table id="S4.T3.11" class="ltx_tabular ltx_centering ltx_align_middle">
<tbody>
<tr id="S4.T3.6.6" class="ltx_tr">

class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" style="font-size:90%;">IDM</span></td>
<td id="S4.T3.6.6.8" class="ltx_td ltx_align_center ltx_border_tt"
style="padding: 0.25pt 5.0pt"><span id="S4.T3.6.6.8.1"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" style="font-size:90%;">CL
iters.</span></td>
<td id="S4.T3.1.1.1" class="ltx_td ltx_align_center ltx_border_tt"
style="padding: 0.25pt 5.0pt"><span id="S4.T3.1.1.1.1"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" style="font-size:90%;">NC</span><span
id="S4.T3.1.1.1.2" class="ltx_text" style="font-size:90%;"> </span><span
class="math inline">↑</span></td>
<td id="S4.T3.2.2.2" class="ltx_td ltx_align_center ltx_border_tt"

<div id="S4.SS4.SSS0.Px2" class="section ltx_paragraph">

##### Temporal design of IDM.

<div id="S4.SS4.SSS0.Px2.p1" class="ltx_para">

Table <a href="#S4.T4" class="ltx_ref"
title="Table 4 ‣ Temporal design of IDM. ‣ 4.4 Ablation study ‣ 4 Experiments ‣ IDOL: Inverse-Dynamics-Guided Future Prediction for End-to-End Autonomous Driving"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">4</span></a> compares different temporal
inputs for IDM. The two-frame design directly decodes adjacent BEV
transitions, while the four-frame variant uses a longer window that may
dilute local transition cues for immediate refinement. The better
performance of the two-frame setting shows that adjacent transitions
provide a more compact and effective basis for planning-oriented motion
refinement.

</div>

<div id="S4.SS4.SSS0.Px3" class="section ltx_paragraph">

##### Effect of spatial and global dynamics branches.

<div id="S4.SS4.SSS0.Px3.p1" class="ltx_para">

Table <a href="#S4.T5" class="ltx_ref"
title="Table 5 ‣ Effect of spatial and global dynamics branches. ‣ 4.4 Ablation study ‣ 4 Experiments ‣ IDOL: Inverse-Dynamics-Guided Future Prediction for End-to-End Autonomous Driving"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">5</span></a> analyzes the dual-branch
design. The global branch captures holistic transition patterns but
lacks spatial selectivity, while the spatial branch preserves local
transition evidence but lacks global calibration. Combining both
branches provides spatially selective and globally calibrated
future-to-planning feedback, achieving the best overall PDMS.

</div>

<figure id="S4.T5" class="ltx_table">
<table id="S4.T5.6" class="ltx_tabular ltx_centering ltx_align_middle">

class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold" style="font-size:90%;">Dynamics
Branch</span></td>

<div id="S4.SS5" class="section ltx_subsection">

### <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_subsection">4.5 </span>Visualization

<div id="S4.SS5.p1" class="ltx_para">

To better understand inverse-dynamics-guided refinement, we visualize a
representative NAVSIM navtest case in
Fig. <a href="#S4.F3" class="ltx_ref"
title="Figure 3 ‣ 4.5 Visualization ‣ 4 Experiments ‣ IDOL: Inverse-Dynamics-Guided Future Prediction for End-to-End Autonomous Driving"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">3</span></a>. Panels (a)–(c) show the
latent BEV change, the corresponding IDM spatial response, and the
refinement-query attention, respectively. The white contour marks
high-change regions extracted from Panel (a). The visualization shows
that IDM indeed concentrates on the motion-informative parts within the
latent BEV change regions, rather than unrelated background variations,
and the query attends to these motion-aware cues during refinement.
Panel (d) compares the original, refined, and expert trajectories, where
the refined trajectory better aligns with the expert, suggesting that
IDM converts latent future transitions into actionable planning updates.
More qualitative results are provided in
Appendix <a href="#A5" class="ltx_ref"
title="Appendix E Additional qualitative visualization ‣ IDOL: Inverse-Dynamics-Guided Future Prediction for End-to-End Autonomous Driving"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">E</span></a>.

</div>

<figure id="S4.F3" class="ltx_figure">
<img src="2605.31476v1/x3.png" id="S4.F3.g1"
class="ltx_graphics ltx_centering ltx_img_landscape" width="830"
height="205" alt="Refer to caption" />
<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure">Figure 3:
</span>Qualitative visualization of inverse-dynamics-guided refinement
on the NAVSIM navtest split.</figcaption>
</figure>

</div>

</div>

<div id="S5" class="section ltx_section">

## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_section">5 </span>Conclusion

<div id="S5.p1" class="ltx_para">

In this work, we proposed IDOL, an inverse-dynamics-guided future
prediction framework for end-to-end autonomous driving. By decoding
adjacent latent BEV futures, IDOL converts predicted scene evolution
into transition-aware motion cues and refines trajectory planning
through a lightweight closed-loop process. This explicitly bridges world
modeling and executable motion generation, making future prediction more
actionable for planning. Experiments on NAVSIM v1 and NAVSIM v2 show
that IDOL achieves state-of-the-art performance on major benchmarks,
including navtest, navhard stage-1, and the two-stage navhard
evaluation, demonstrating the effectiveness and generality of
inverse-dynamics-guided future reasoning.

</div>

</div>

<div id="A1" class="section ltx_appendix">

## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_appendix">Appendix A </span>Implementation details

<div id="A1.SS0.SSS0.Px1" class="section ltx_paragraph">

##### Hyperparameters.

<div id="A1.SS0.SSS0.Px1.p1" class="ltx_para">

Table <a href="#A1.T6" class="ltx_ref"
title="Table 6 ‣ Hyperparameters. ‣ Appendix A Implementation details ‣ IDOL: Inverse-Dynamics-Guided Future Prediction for End-to-End Autonomous Driving"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">6</span></a> summarizes the key
hyperparameters used in IDOL. The trajectory-anchor vocabulary is
constructed offline and kept fixed during training and evaluation. For
ablation studies, all settings are unchanged unless the corresponding
component is explicitly modified.

<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_table">Table 6:
</span>Hyperparameters of IDOL.</figcaption>
</figure>

</div>

<div id="A1.SS0.SSS0.Px2" class="section ltx_paragraph">

##### Inverse dynamics model.

<div id="A1.SS0.SSS0.Px2.p1" class="ltx_para">

IDOL adopts a lightweight MLP-based inverse dynamics model to convert
adjacent imagined BEV states into motion-aware transition cues. Given
two consecutive latent BEV states, the model first forms a transition
representation by concatenating their query features along the channel
dimension. A three-stage MLP transition encoder is then applied: an
initial fusion stage compresses the paired-state representation into the
latent feature space, followed by residual MLP transformations that
refine query-wise transition patterns while preserving the original
spatial layout. The resulting query-wise representation can be viewed as
a *spatial dynamics map*, which captures local motion-relevant changes
across BEV queries. IDOL further aggregates this spatial dynamics map
into a compact *global dynamics feature*, which serves as a
trajectory-level motion condition for modulating the anchor-conditioned
ego planning feature through an MLN layer before trajectory offset
prediction. This design keeps the inverse dynamics branch lightweight
while allowing the planner to exploit both local transition patterns and
global motion context from adjacent imagined futures.

</div>

</div>

<div id="A1.SS0.SSS0.Px3" class="section ltx_paragraph">

##### Loss configuration.

<div id="A1.SS0.SSS0.Px3.p1" class="ltx_para">

IDOL is optimized with trajectory offset regression, imitation reward
supervision, simulator-metric reward supervision, and BEV semantic
supervision. The loss terms follow the same definitions as in the main
paper. Table <a href="#A1.T7" class="ltx_ref"
title="Table 7 ‣ Loss configuration. ‣ Appendix A Implementation details ‣ IDOL: Inverse-Dynamics-Guided Future Prediction for End-to-End Autonomous Driving"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">7</span></a> lists the role of each loss
term for reproducibility.

</div>

<figure id="A1.T7" class="ltx_table">
<table id="A1.T7.5" class="ltx_tabular ltx_centering ltx_align_middle">
<tbody>
<tr id="A1.T7.5.6" class="ltx_tr">

<div id="A2" class="section ltx_appendix">

## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_appendix">Appendix B </span>Benchmark and metrics details

<div id="A2.SS0.SSS0.Px1" class="section ltx_paragraph">

##### Benchmark.

<div id="A2.SS0.SSS0.Px1.p1" class="ltx_para">

All models are trained on the NAVSIM training split. We evaluate IDOL on
the NAVSIM benchmark. The <span id="A2.SS0.SSS0.Px1.p1.1.1"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic">navtest</span> split is the standard
test split used to evaluate closed-loop planning performance under
common urban driving scenarios. The <span id="A2.SS0.SSS0.Px1.p1.1.2"
class="ltx_text ltx_font_italic">navhard</span> split is a more
challenging subset designed to stress-test planners under difficult
cases such as complex interactions, stricter route following, and
long-tail driving conditions. For NAVSIM v2, navhard is evaluated under
the pseudo-simulation protocol, including the stage-1-only setting and
the two-stage setting. The former measures the quality of the initially
generated trajectory, while the latter further evaluates the trajectory
after pseudo-simulation rollout and therefore reflects closed-loop
robustness more directly. Since point-cloud inputs are unavailable in
the second stage of navhard, we replace the LiDAR branch with Latent
TransFuser (LTF) for this setting, which preserves the latent fusion
pipeline without relying on inaccessible LiDAR observations.

</div>

</div>

<div id="A2.SS0.SSS0.Px2" class="section ltx_paragraph">

##### PDMS.

<div id="A2.SS0.SSS0.Px2.p1" class="ltx_para">

For NAVSIM v1, we report the Predictive Driver Model Score (PDMS), which
evaluates trajectory quality after non-reactive simulation. PDMS uses
no-collision (NC) and drivable area compliance (DAC) as multiplicative
safety constraints, and combines ego progress (EP), time-to-collision
within bound (TTC), and comfort (C) as weighted quality terms:

|  |  |  |  |
|----|----|----|----|
|  | 
``` math
\mathrm{PDMS}=\mathrm{NC}\cdot\mathrm{DAC}\cdot\frac{5\mathrm{EP}+5\mathrm{TTC}+2\mathrm{C}}{12}.
``` |  | <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_equation ltx_align_right">(13)</span> |

All component metrics are normalized to $`[0,1]`$, and the final
reported PDMS is averaged over all evaluated scenarios and expressed as
a percentage.

</div>

</div>

<div id="A2.SS0.SSS0.Px3" class="section ltx_paragraph">

##### EPDMS.

<div id="A2.SS0.SSS0.Px3.p1" class="ltx_para">

For NAVSIM v2, we report the Extended Predictive Driver Model Score
(EPDMS). Compared with PDMS, EPDMS adds driving direction compliance
(DDC) and traffic light compliance (TLC) as multiplicative
rule-compliance constraints, and further introduces lane keeping (LK),
history comfort (HC), and extended comfort (EC) into the weighted
quality term. Following the NAVSIM v2 metric definition, we write the
filtered value of each metric as $`\tilde{m}`$, where human-trajectory
violations are filtered to avoid false-positive penalties. EPDMS is
computed as

|  |  |  |  |
|----|----|----|----|
|  | 
``` math
\mathrm{EPDMS}=\tilde{\mathrm{NC}}\cdot\tilde{\mathrm{DAC}}\cdot\tilde{\mathrm{DDC}}\cdot\tilde{\mathrm{TLC}}\cdot\frac{5\tilde{\mathrm{EP}}+5\tilde{\mathrm{TTC}}+2\tilde{\mathrm{LK}}+2\tilde{\mathrm{HC}}+2\tilde{\mathrm{EC}}}{16}.
``` |  | <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_equation ltx_align_right">(14)</span> |

EPDMS therefore extends PDMS from basic safety and progress evaluation
to stricter rule compliance, lane keeping, and pseudo-closed-loop
consistency, making it more suitable for NAVSIM v2 and the challenging
navhard split.

</div>

</div>

</div>

<div id="A3" class="section ltx_appendix">

## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_appendix">Appendix C </span>Additional evaluation on NAVSIM v2

<div id="A3.p1" class="ltx_para">

We further report extended NAVSIM v2 results on both the standard
navtest split and the more challenging navhard stage-1 setting. For
completeness, several additional reproduced baseline results are
collected from publicly available
papers\[<a href="#bib.bib84" class="ltx_ref"
title="Irl-vla: training an vision-language-action policy via reward world model">26</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib38" class="ltx_ref"
title="MindDrive: an all-in-one framework bridging world models and vision-language model for end-to-end autonomous driving">54</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib85" class="ltx_ref"
title="DiffSemanticFusion: semantic raster bev fusion for autonomous driving via online map diffusion">56</a>,
<a href="#bib.bib54" class="ltx_ref"
title="GuideFlow: constraint-guided flow matching for planning in end-to-end autonomous driving">45</a>\].
As shown in Table <a href="#A3.T8" class="ltx_ref"
title="Table 8 ‣ Appendix C Additional evaluation on NAVSIM v2 ‣ IDOL: Inverse-Dynamics-Guided Future Prediction for End-to-End Autonomous Driving"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">8</span></a>, IDOL achieves an EPDMS of
89.6 on navtest, ranking first among learned methods and remaining
competitive with the human agent, while maintaining consistently strong
safety, rule-compliance, and comfort-related metrics.

</div>

<div id="A3.p2" class="ltx_para">

On the navhard stage-1 protocol in
Table <a href="#A3.T9" class="ltx_ref"
title="Table 9 ‣ Appendix C Additional evaluation on NAVSIM v2 ‣ IDOL: Inverse-Dynamics-Guided Future Prediction for End-to-End Autonomous Driving"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">9</span></a>, IDOL obtains 76.2 EPDMS,
outperforming prior learned planners including Hydra-MDP and WoTE. These
results indicate that the proposed inverse-dynamics-guided future
reasoning does not only improve performance under standard evaluation,
but also provides stable gains under harder long-tail scenarios,
demonstrating the robustness and generality of IDOL across different
NAVSIM v2 settings.

</div>

<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_table">Table 8: </span>
Performance comparison on the NAVSIM v2 navtest split using extended
closed-loop metrics. The best and second-best results among learned
methods are highlighted in bold and underlined, respectively.
Superscripts indicate methods using additional training signals or
settings beyond standard imitation learning: <sup>∗</sup> denotes
VLA/VLM-based methods, <sup>†</sup> denotes reinforcement learning,
reward-guided training, or reinforcement fine-tuning, and <sup>‡</sup>
denotes extra synthetic, pseudo-expert, or QA-style data.</figcaption>
</figure>

<div id="A4" class="section ltx_appendix">

## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_appendix">Appendix D </span>Additional ablation studies

<div id="A4.p1" class="ltx_para">

We provide additional ablation studies to further examine the design
choices in IDOL. These experiments focus on two questions : whether
learned inverse dynamics provides more effective transition-aware
guidance than simpler future-state alternatives, and how global
inverse-dynamics features should be fused with the refined planning
query.

</div>

<div id="A4.SS0.SSS0.Px1" class="section ltx_paragraph">

##### Ablation on transition-aware future modeling.

<div id="A4.SS0.SSS0.Px1.p1" class="ltx_para">

We first compare different ways of using imagined future BEV states for
planning refinement. The future-state-only variant uses predicted future
BEV features without explicitly modeling transitions between adjacent
states. The latent-difference variant replaces the learned inverse
dynamics model with a direct feature difference between adjacent future
BEV states. In contrast, IDOL adopts a learned inverse dynamics model to
decode transition-aware motion cues from adjacent latent futures. As
shown in Table <a href="#A4.T10" class="ltx_ref"
title="Table 10 ‣ Ablation on transition-aware future modeling. ‣ Appendix D Additional ablation studies ‣ IDOL: Inverse-Dynamics-Guided Future Prediction for End-to-End Autonomous Driving"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">10</span></a>, both transition-based
variants outperform the future-state-only baseline, confirming that
future-state changes contain useful motion-relevant information for
planning. This verifies that IDOL benefits not merely from accessing
imagined future states, but from learning an explicit
transition-to-motion mapping that converts future evolution into
actionable planning guidance.

</div>

<figure id="A4.T10" class="ltx_table">
<table id="A4.T10.6" class="ltx_tabular ltx_centering ltx_align_middle">
<tbody>
<tr id="A4.T10.6.6" class="ltx_tr">
<td id="A4.T10.6.6.7" class="ltx_td ltx_align_left ltx_border_tt"
style="padding-top: 0.25pt; padding-bottom: 0.25pt"><span
id="A4.T10.6.6.7.1" class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold"
style="font-size:90%;">Transition Modeling</span></td>
<td id="A4.T10.1.1.1" class="ltx_td ltx_align_center ltx_border_tt"
style="padding-top: 0.25pt; padding-bottom: 0.25pt"><span
id="A4.T10.1.1.1.1" class="ltx_text ltx_font_bold"
style="font-size:90%;">NC</span><span id="A4.T10.1.1.1.2"

Ablation of transition-aware future modeling strategies on the NAVSIM
navtest split. Future state only uses imagined future BEV states without
explicit transition decoding, while latent difference replaces the
inverse dynamics model with direct adjacent-feature
differences.</figcaption>
</figure>

<div id="A4.SS0.SSS0.Px2" class="section ltx_paragraph">

##### Ablation on global dynamics fusion.

<div id="A4.SS0.SSS0.Px2.p1" class="ltx_para">

We further study how to fuse the global dynamics feature produced by the
inverse dynamics model. In the inverse-dynamics-guided refinement
network, the global dynamics feature provides holistic calibration for
the query refined by spatial transition evidence. We compare three
simple fusion strategies: additive fusion, which directly injects the
projected global feature into the query; concat-MLP fusion, which learns
a nonlinear fusion from the concatenated query and global feature; and
the MLN-based fusion used in IDOL. As shown in
Table <a href="#A4.T11" class="ltx_ref"
title="Table 11 ‣ Ablation on global dynamics fusion. ‣ Appendix D Additional ablation studies ‣ IDOL: Inverse-Dynamics-Guided Future Prediction for End-to-End Autonomous Driving"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">11</span></a>, all variants obtain strong
performance, indicating that the global inverse-dynamics cue is
consistently beneficial for query refinement. These results verify that
IDOL’s performance is not only due to using inverse-dynamics features,
but also benefits from an effective query-calibration mechanism that
better integrates global transition information into planning.

</div>

<figure id="A4.T11" class="ltx_table">
<table id="A4.T11.6" class="ltx_tabular ltx_centering ltx_align_middle">

<div id="A5" class="section ltx_appendix">

## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_appendix">Appendix E </span>Additional qualitative visualization

<div id="A5.p1" class="ltx_para">

To further demonstrate the effectiveness of our proposed method, we
expand the qualitative visualization experiments with additional
representative driving scenarios.
Figure <a href="#A5.F4" class="ltx_ref"
title="Figure 4 ‣ Appendix E Additional qualitative visualization ‣ IDOL: Inverse-Dynamics-Guided Future Prediction for End-to-End Autonomous Driving"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">4</span></a> presents representative
visualization results on the NAVSIM navtest split under three driving
maneuvers, including straight driving, left turn, and right turn. Each
example contains four panels that illustrate how the proposed IDM module
extracts spatial dynamics from latent BEV transitions and uses them to
refine the trajectory prediction.

</div>

<div id="A5.p2" class="ltx_para">

Panel (a) shows the latent BEV change between two adjacent imagined BEV
states produced by the world model. The highlighted regions indicate
where the scene representation changes most significantly from frame
$`t`$ to frame $`t+1`$ . Therefore, this panel serves as the reference
evidence for spatially localized future dynamics, such as
ego-motion-induced changes, nearby traffic participants, and lane-level
scene evolution.

</div>

<div id="A5.p3" class="ltx_para">

Panel (b) visualizes the spatial dynamics inferred by the IDM from the
same adjacent BEV pair. Instead of directly displaying a semantic map,
this panel shows the inverse-dynamics response over BEV queries. The
white contour marks the high-change regions extracted from Panel (a),
and is overlaid as a reference for comparing IDM responses with latent
BEV transitions. The regions with stronger activation correspond to
locations where IDM considers the transition between the two BEV states
informative for explaining the underlying motion. The visual consistency
between Panel (a) and Panel (b) suggests that IDM is not responding to
arbitrary static background features, but is sensitive to the spatial
regions where the latent scene actually evolves.

</div>

<div id="A5.p4" class="ltx_para">

Panel (c) further shows the attention distribution from the trajectory
query to the IDM spatial dynamics. While Panel (b) indicates where
inverse-dynamics information is extracted, Panel (c) reveals which
dynamic regions are actually attended to by the refinement query. The
concentration of attention around the changing BEV regions demonstrates
that the refinement process actively uses the IDM-derived spatial
evidence, rather than treating IDM as an isolated auxiliary branch.

</div>

<div id="A5.p5" class="ltx_para">

Panel (d) compares the original trajectory, the expert trajectory, and
the refined trajectory. The original trajectory is decoded before
applying the IDM-based closed-loop refinement, while the refined
trajectory is decoded after the query has absorbed inverse-dynamics
information through the IDM attention and fusion modules. Across
straight, left-turn, and right-turn cases, the refined trajectory is
visually better aligned with the expert trajectory, showing that the
IDM-guided update helps correct the initial prediction toward a more
plausible future motion.

</div>

<div id="A5.p6" class="ltx_para">

Overall, these qualitative results provide direct visual evidence for
the effectiveness of the IDM module. The latent BEV transition
identifies where the future scene changes, the IDM spatial response
recovers dynamics from those changing regions, and the query attention
confirms that such information is used by the planner. The resulting
trajectory refinement illustrates the practical value of this mechanism:
IDM helps the model focus on motion-relevant spatial changes and
improves the final trajectory prediction in different driving maneuvers.
This supports our hypothesis that inverse-dynamics reasoning provides a
meaningful bridge between latent world-model prediction and
trajectory-level decision refinement.

</div>

<figure id="A5.F4" class="ltx_figure">
<div class="ltx_flex_figure">
<div class="ltx_flex_cell ltx_flex_size_1">
<img src="2605.31476v1/x4.png" id="A5.F4.g1"
class="ltx_graphics ltx_centering ltx_figure_panel ltx_img_landscape"
width="660" height="166" alt="Refer to caption" />
</div>

</div>

</div>

</div>
</div>
<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure">Figure 4:
</span>Qualitative visualization of inverse-dynamics refinement under
three representative driving maneuvers on the NAVSIM navtest split. From
top to bottom: straight driving, left turn, and right turn. In each row,
panels (a)–(d) show the latent BEV change, the IDM dynamics inferred
from adjacent BEV states, the IDM attention on dynamic queries, and the
resulting refined trajectory, respectively.</figcaption>
</figure>

</div>

<div id="A6" class="section ltx_appendix">

## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_appendix">Appendix F </span>Failure Cases

<div id="A6.p1" class="ltx_para">

We further analyze two representative failure cases on navtest, as shown
in Fig  <a href="#A6.F5" class="ltx_ref"
title="Figure 5 ‣ Appendix F Failure Cases ‣ IDOL: Inverse-Dynamics-Guided Future Prediction for End-to-End Autonomous Driving"><span
class="ltx_text ltx_ref_tag">5</span></a>. Each row contains the
front-view camera image and the corresponding BEV trajectory comparison.
The refined trajectory denotes the final prediction after IDM-based
refinement, and the expert trajectory is used as the reference.

</div>

<div id="A6.p2" class="ltx_para">

In the first case, the ego vehicle approaches a turning area with
ambiguous road geometry. The refined trajectory deviates clearly from
the expert trajectory and follows an incorrect spatial direction. This
indicates that when the model fails to infer the correct maneuver
intention, IDM refinement alone may not fully recover the desired path.

</div>

<div id="A6.p3" class="ltx_para">

In the second case, the scene involves a complex intersection or
roundabout-like structure. Although the refined trajectory captures part
of the motion trend, it remains spatially offset from the expert
trajectory. This suggests that topology-sensitive scenarios still
require more precise reasoning about lane connectivity, road curvature,
and maneuver timing.

</div>

<div id="A6.p4" class="ltx_para">

These cases show that IDM provides useful dynamic cues, but its
effectiveness depends on whether such cues can be correctly fused with
scene topology and trajectory intent. Future work may improve these
limitations with stronger topology-aware constraints and
uncertainty-aware refinement.

</div>

<figure id="A6.F5" class="ltx_figure">
<img src="2605.31476v1/x7.png" id="A6.F5.g1"
class="ltx_graphics ltx_centering ltx_img_square" width="830"
height="692" alt="Refer to caption" />
<figcaption><span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_figure">Figure 5:
</span>Representative failure cases on navtest.</figcaption>
</figure>

</div>

<div id="A7" class="section ltx_appendix">

## <span class="ltx_tag ltx_tag_appendix">Appendix G </span>Limitations and future work

<div id="A7.p1" class="ltx_para">

Although IDOL shows strong performance across NAVSIM benchmarks, several
limitations remain.

</div>

<div id="A7.SS0.SSS0.Px1" class="section ltx_paragraph">

##### Model capacity.

<div id="A7.SS0.SSS0.Px1.p1" class="ltx_para">

Our inverse dynamics model is designed to be lightweight, which keeps
the framework efficient but may limit its ability to capture more
complex long-horizon interactions and subtle motion patterns. Future
work could scale the IDM with larger temporal contexts, stronger
transition modeling, or pretraining on broader driving sequences.

</div>

</div>

<div id="A7.SS0.SSS0.Px2" class="section ltx_paragraph">

##### Dependence on latent future prediction.

<div id="A7.SS0.SSS0.Px2.p1" class="ltx_para">

IDOL relies on the quality of the imagined latent BEV futures produced
by the world model. When future prediction is inaccurate in highly
ambiguous or rare scenarios, the decoded inverse-dynamics cues may also
become less reliable. Improving the robustness of latent world modeling
is therefore an important direction.

</div>

</div>

<div id="A7.SS0.SSS0.Px3" class="section ltx_paragraph">

##### Fixed refinement strategy.

<div id="A7.SS0.SSS0.Px3.p1" class="ltx_para">

The current closed-loop refinement uses a fixed refinement setting,
while different scenes may require different levels of future-aware
reasoning. Adaptive refinement strategies could further improve
stability and avoid unnecessary iterative updates.

</div>

</div>

<div id="A7.SS0.SSS0.Px4" class="section ltx_paragraph">

##### Broader evaluation.

<div id="A7.SS0.SSS0.Px4.p1" class="ltx_para">

Although our experiments cover both standard and challenging NAVSIM
settings, future work will further evaluate inverse-dynamics-guided
planning in more interactive, long-tail, and real-world driving
scenarios.

</div>

<div class="ltx_pagination ltx_role_newpage">

</div>

</div>

</div>

</div>
